    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1627a4bdb8624f4fac48b48b0d5db25a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'def3be34-2cad-4fc0-abfc-40d5e9a3722c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d90367819" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The sub-10&nbsp;nm metal-based nanomaterials (SMNs) show great potential for the electrochemical energy storage field. However, their ion storage capacity and stability suffer from severe agglomeration and interface problems. Herein, a universal strategy is reported to synthesize a wide range of SMNs (e.g., metal, nitride, oxide, and sulfides) embedded in free-standing carbon foam (SMN/FC-F) composite electrodes by crossing the interfacial confinement of NaCl self-assembly with the thermal-mechanical coupling of powder metallurgy. The pressure-enhanced NaCl self-assembly interfacial confinement is greatly beneficial to preventing SMN agglomeration and promoting SMNs embedded in FC-F which originate from the welding of carbon nanosheets. They are confirmed via a series of advanced characterizations including X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy, and spherical aberration-corrected scanning transmission electron microscopy, with theoretical computations. Benefiting from the unique structure, SMNs/FC-F delivers ultrafast and stable ion-storage kinetics. As a proof-of-concept demonstration, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F shows excellent ion storage kinetics and superior long-term cycling performance for ion storage (e.g., Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F/C//MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F sodium-ion batteries exhibit a high reversible capacity of 185&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a decay rate of 0.05% per cycle.). This work provides a new opportunity to design and fabricate promising SMN-based free-standing working electrodes for electrochemical energy storage and conversion applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202307209-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202307209-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Electrochemical energy conversion and storage devices (EECSDs) play a crucial role in carbon neutrality because they promote the utilization of intermittent renewable energy and help the capture and reuse of CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0001_R_d90367811e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater. Electrochem. Energy Rev. Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Developing catalysts or electrode materials with good reaction kinetics has attracted abundant attention. Emerging sub-10&nbsp;nm metal-based nanomaterials (SMNs, 1–10&nbsp;nm) have been considered as one kind of ideal catalysts or electrode materials for EECSDs due to their unique size effects and excellent physical and chemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0002_R_d90367811e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev. ACS Nano Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, their practical application is limited by the following major two problems: the lack of efficient, low-cost, and scalable preparation methods,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0003_R_d90367811e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Annu. Rev. Mater. Sci. Nature">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the poor reaction kinetics and stability attributed to agglomeration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0004_R_d90367811e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater. ACS Nano Energy Storage Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing a green and universal strategy to prepare stable SMNs is of great importance.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, incorporating SMNs with carbon substrates has been widely used and demonstrated to be an effective strategy to address their agglomeration and poor stability issues.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0005_R_d90367811e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Carbon Nat. Nanotechnol. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the free-standingporous carbon structure has been considered an ideal substrate due to its high stability, good conductivity, and good mechanical property.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0006_R_d90367811e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nano Today Nano Today">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the SMN/free-standing carbon foam (SMN/FC-F) electrodes have great potential to show fast and stable electrochemical reaction kinetics, especially for electrochemical ion storage devices. Moreover, the proposed SMN/FC-F electrodes have 100% active material utilization and don't need additional homogenate, coating, and drying preparation processes, greatly promoting the batteries’ energy density and reducing the cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0007_R_d90367811e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>For preparing SMN/FC-Fs, the extensively employed methods involve two main categories: i) preparing FC-Fs through template-assisted chemical vapor deposition or pyrolysis of carbon source in advance, and then growing SMNs on their surface;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0008_R_d90367811e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Mater. Nat. Commun. Science">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ii) preparing SMNs through wet chemical methods in advance and then loading them on/in FC-Fs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0009_R_d90367811e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Nat. Commun. Acc. Chem. Res.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the above asynchronous preparation methods have serious disadvantages, including the time-consuming process, low yield, and poorly controllable dispersion and interfacial interaction. Bottom-up in situ synchronous construction of SMNs and FC-Fs shows great potential to address the above issues. However, it is extremely difficult for controllable synthesis of SMN/FC-Fs in the same environment since they are usually synthesized at different temperatures (&lt;300&nbsp;°C for FC-F and &gt;800&nbsp;°C for SMN).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0003_R_d90367811e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Annu. Rev. Mater. Sci. Nature">3</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0006_R_d90367811e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nano Today Nano Today">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the key challenge in producing promising SMN/FC-Fs is how to synchronously make SMNs uniformly distributed in FC-Fs by a cheap and green preparation strategy.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, by crossing the interfacial confinement of NaCl self-assembly<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0010_R_d90367811e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Nano">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with the thermal-mechanical coupling of powder metallurgy hot pressing,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0011_R_d90367811e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we prepared a series of SMNs, including 0D metal (Sn), nitride (Fe<sub>3</sub>N), oxide (MoO<sub>2</sub>), sulfide (V<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>) nanoparticles (1–10&nbsp;nm), and 2D sulfide (WS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>) nanosheets (1–10&nbsp;nm and single layer), which are monodispersed and embedded in FC-Fs through robust interfacial interaction. The unique structure endows fast electron/ion transfer and durably structural integrity of the overall electrode, leading to fast and stable ion storage reaction kinetics. Taking sub-10&nbsp;nm MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet embedded in FC-Fs (MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-Fs) as a proof-of-concept demonstration, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-Fs exhibits a highly reversible conversion reaction as an anode of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), resulting in excellent long-term cycling stability (277.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 5000 cycles with near 100% retention). This is better than those of mostly reported MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode materials. The prepared SMN-based free-standing working electrodes are promising in a wide application range besides ion storage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202307209-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202307209-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307209-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202307209-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis and Characterization of SMN/FC-Fs</h3>
            
            <p>The general preparation process of SMN/FC-Fs is schematically depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307209-fig-0001">1a</a>. The NaCl template, citric acid, and metal salts were first dissolved in distilled water. After the freeze-drying process, the NaCl cubes happened self-assembly, while the citric acid and metal compound precursors (CA-MCPs) were formed into gels and uniformly coated on the surface of the NaCl cubes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0010_R_d90367811e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Nano">10</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0012_R_d90367811e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Nano">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the CA-MCP network was distributed at the interfaces of the NaCl self-assembly (NaCl@CA-MCP-N). The obtained NaCl@CA-MCP-N powders were pressed into a tightly packed mass in a round graphite mold (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information) and then hot-pressing sintered with an external pressure of 30&nbsp;MPa. The pressure-enhanced interfacial confinement of the NaCl self-assembly plays a key role in the pyrolysis of internal CA-MCP-N gels. The citric acid network and metal compound were respectively transformed into FC-Fs and SNMs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information). After washing in water to remove the NaCl template, SMNs embedded in FC-Fs were obtained (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the foams can be cut into many electrodes with proper size (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202307209-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a3195340-04a4-4fc7-b5bf-4e90eaa71ef2/adma202307209-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a3195340-04a4-4fc7-b5bf-4e90eaa71ef2/adma202307209-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b7cd52c2-3803-4f00-b8c9-ee63f838eb50/adma202307209-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a3195340-04a4-4fc7-b5bf-4e90eaa71ef2/adma202307209-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307209-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307209" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Preparation and characterization of SMN/FC-Fs. a) Schematic illustration of the preparation processes for SMN/FC-Fs, b) the corresponding HRTEM images (Scale bar: 20&nbsp;nm), and c) the average sizes of metal and metal compounds in prepared SMN/FC-Fs and LMN/PC-Ps.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To demonstrate the universality of this strategy, a series of SMNs, including 0D metal (Sn), nitride (Fe<sub>3</sub>N), oxide (MoO<sub>2</sub>), sulfide (V<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>) nanoparticles, and 2D sulfide (WS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>) nanosheets, which are monodispersed and embedded in FC-Fs, obtaining Sn/FC-F, Fe<sub>3</sub>N/FC-F, MoO<sub>2</sub>/FC-F, V<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/FC-F, and WS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F, respectively. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0001">1b</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S7</a> (Supporting Information) exhibit their high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images, X-Ray diffraction (XRD) patterns, digital photographs (inset), and scanning electron microscope (SEM) images. All of them show free-standing foam morphologies, in which ultrafine nanoparticles or nanosheets are embedded in carbon. The size distribution histograms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0001">1c</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S7</a>, Supporting Information) exhibit that all the SMNs have ultrafine average sizes (2.8–5.9&nbsp;nm). In addition, the WS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets respectively have a single layer in WS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F, according to the HRTEM images and XRD patterns.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0013_R_d90367811e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>For comparison, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), the obtained NaCl@CA-MCP-N powders were calcinated in Ar gas flow at atmospheric pressure. The obtained materials exhibit traditional powder morphology, in which large-size metal-based nanomaterials are anchored on the surface of porous carbon powders (LMN/PC-Ps). As shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), XRD patterns, SEM images, and TEM images confirm Sn/PC-P, Fe<sub>3</sub>N/PC-P, MoO<sub>2</sub>/PC-P, V<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/PC-P, WS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P. The size distribution histograms display that all metal compound nanomaterials have large average sizes (21.2–355.3&nbsp;nm).</p>
            
            <p>The above results indicate that the high pressure acting on the interfaces of the self-assembly NaCl plays a key role in the synchronous formation of both SMNs and FC-Fs. The pressure-enhanced interfacial confinement of the NaCl self-assembly is greatly beneficial to blocking SMNs agglomeration and promoting carbon welding into FC-F, which makes the SMNs embedded in the ultrathin carbon nanosheet of FC-Fs through strong interfacial interaction.</p>
            
            <p>To further investigate the mechanism of external pressure on the formation of the free-standing carbon foam (FC-F) structure, the pure FC-F and porous carbon powder (PC-P) were prepared by calcining the citric acid network distributed at the interfaces of the NaCl self-assembly (NaCl@CA-N) powders at an external pressure of 30&nbsp;MPa (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a>, Supporting Information) and atmospheric pressure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. The FC-Fs without SMNs remain free-standing foam morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b–d</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, the FC-F has a large number of Y-type contact junctions between carbon, which are welded by two adjacent carbon nanosheets with a large area under high-pressure conditions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15e</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">g</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0011_R_d90367811e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the ultrathin carbon porous network forms a continuous and whole foam morphology with good mechanical properties. For comparison, the PC-Ps exhibit a randomly stacked porous carbon powder morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b,c</a>, Supporting Information). The carbon nano-boxes in PC-Ps are relatively independent and even separated, without the formation of interfacial welding junction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d–g</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the above analysis, the high-pressure field leads to the interfacial welding phenomenon between carbon nanosheets, enabling free-standing porous carbon foam formation.</p>
            
            <p>In the previous literature, the electrical Joule welding temperature of graphene layers without the assistance of solder is more than 1200&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0014_R_d90367811e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Mater. Today Nano">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The welding temperature in this work is as low as 650&nbsp;°C. To understand the welding mechanism of carbon nanosheets at the NaCl cube interfaces under an external pressure of 30&nbsp;MPa, the molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were performed based on a model depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307209-fig-0002">2a</a> displays the 10&nbsp;ps simulation results at the loading temperature of 650&nbsp;°C with different exerting external pressures of 0.1&nbsp;MPa (atmospheric pressure), 10, 20, and 30&nbsp;MPa (corresponding to the parameters in the experiment).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0011_R_d90367811e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The obtained number of bonding carbon atoms of adjacent graphene layers with bond lengths of 2.2–2.9&nbsp;Å was quantitatively counted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0002">2b</a>. With increasing the exerting external pressure, both the distribution sizes of bond lengths between adjacent graphene layers and the spacings between NaCl cubes reduce. Notably, under the pressure of 30&nbsp;MPa, the bond length of bonding carbon atoms shows a distinct peak at a short bond value of around 2.45&nbsp;Å, indicating the carbon atoms that are bonded by covalent-like π–π bonds between adjacent graphene layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0011_R_d90367811e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0015_R_d90367811e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. - Eur. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high external pressure significantly reduces the distance between the formed carbon nanosheets, helping to decrease the critical temperature of carbon welding to below 1200&nbsp;°C. On the other hand, the spacing size between NaCl cubic reduced to only 13.9&nbsp;Å under 30&nbsp;MPa pressure, which is greatly beneficial to blocking the diffusion and agglomeration of metal compound nanomaterials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0016_R_d90367811e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun. Small">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the small molecule organic and carbon substrate helps to lock in the SMNs and block agglomeration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0004_R_d90367811e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater. ACS Nano Energy Storage Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0017_R_d90367811e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Science Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They greatly increase the critical temperature of SMNs of aggregation to above 300&nbsp;°C. Therefore, under the assistance of high external pressure of 30&nbsp;MPa between the NaCl cube interfaces, the adjacent carbon layers happen to weld, and the SMNs are in situ embedded in carbon layers at an intermediate temperature of 650&nbsp;°C.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202307209-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c0bc7cae-bc22-4160-b9ae-e2ce17adbe40/adma202307209-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c0bc7cae-bc22-4160-b9ae-e2ce17adbe40/adma202307209-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9537c63e-79aa-46cf-b7f1-3fab42b1eb0f/adma202307209-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c0bc7cae-bc22-4160-b9ae-e2ce17adbe40/adma202307209-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307209-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307209" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural defect of FC-F and PC-P. a) MD simulation of the welding process of carbon nanosheets at 10&nbsp;ps and b) the corresponding bonding length statistics at different simulated external pressure. Deconvoluted Raman spectra of c) FC-F and d) PC-P, and e) the corresponding peak intensity ratios of ID/IG. Deconvoluted C 1s fine XPS spectra of f) FC-F and g) PC-P, and h) the corresponding peak intensity ratios of <i>sp<sup>3</sup>/sp<sup>2</sup></i>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further confirm the welding process between carbon nanosheets under high pressure, the structural defects and chemical states of FC-Fs and PC-Ps were analyzed. The Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0002">2c,d</a>) can be deconvoluted into five peaks, including the G band at ≈1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (the crystalline graphitic C–C stretching vibration mode with E<sub>2g</sub> symmetry), the D1 peak at ≈1360&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (the disordered graphitic lattice of edges with A<sub>1g</sub> symmetry), the D2 peak at ≈1640&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (the disordered graphitic lattice of surface graphene layers with E<sub>2g</sub> symmetry), the D3 peak at ≈1500&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (the amorphous carbon), and the D4 peak at ≈1180&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (the disordered graphitic lattice of the stretching vibrations of <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i>–<i>sp<sup>3</sup></i> bonds with A<sub>1g</sub> symmetry).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0018_R_d90367811e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon Carbon">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The higher the ratio of the intensity of the D1, D2, and D4 bands to the intensity of the G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>, <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>I</i><sub>D1</sub>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>I</i><sub>D2</sub>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>I</i><sub>D4</sub>), the higher the defect degree of carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0018_R_d90367811e900" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon Carbon">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the C1s high-resolution X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0002">2f,g</a>) can be deconvoluted into <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i>, <i>sp<sup>3</sup></i>, and C–O three peaks, which are located at the bonding energy of 284.6, 285.0, and 286.0&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0011_R_d90367811e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0019_R_d90367811e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The higher the ratio of the integrated area of <i>sp<sup>3</sup></i> and <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> peaks (<i>sp<sup>3</sup></i>/<i>sp<sup>2</sup></i>), the higher the defect degree of carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0011_R_d90367811e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0019_R_d90367811e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0002">2e,h</a>, both the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> (2.69) and <i>sp<sup>3</sup></i>/<i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> (1.55) ratios of FC-F are smaller than those of PC-P (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is 3.43 and <i>sp<sup>3</sup></i>/<i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> is 1.79), indicating that the FC-F has smaller defect degree than PC-P. The above results further validate that the high pressure at the NaCl cube interfaces significantly decreases the structural defect, promotes structural healing, and constructs a free-standing foam structure.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307209-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202307209-sec-0040-title">2.2 Morphology and Structure of SMN/FC-Fs</h3>
            
            <p>The morphologies and chemical states of SMN/FC-Fs were further studied to reveal their structural advantages. As a proof-of-concept demonstration, the structural information of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-Fs was performed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307209-fig-0003">3</a>. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F exhibits a continuous porous network (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0003">3a</a>),&nbsp;in which the carbon nanosheets are welded together through a large overlapping area (green circles) to form a large number of Y-type contact junctions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0003">3b</a>). The spherical aberration-corrected scanning TEM annular dark field (AC-STEM-ADF) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0003">3c</a>) confirms a honeycomb lattice with a typical lattice spacing of 0.26&nbsp;nm, giving evidence of the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0020_R_d90367811e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To evaluate the porous nature, nitrogen adsorption–desorption measurements were performed, with the results shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F composite is 193.26&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is smaller than that of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P (246.35&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The pore size distribution curves exhibit that MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F has a smaller pore size.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202307209-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ebcc8120-0537-4a8f-9fd6-80b285d8a1dc/adma202307209-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ebcc8120-0537-4a8f-9fd6-80b285d8a1dc/adma202307209-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/257a3b9a-be99-4a53-8bd8-a856e4fd1dc5/adma202307209-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ebcc8120-0537-4a8f-9fd6-80b285d8a1dc/adma202307209-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307209-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307209" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P. a,b) SEM images and c) AC-STEM-ADF image of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F. d) Photographs of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F being compressed and released. e) TGA curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F. f) Mo 3d, and g) S 2p XPS spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The compressive properties were studied in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, the prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F can recover to its original shape without mechanical fracture even after loading a high compressive stress of ≈63.7&nbsp;kPa. The height change is only 4% after compressing and releasing. The above tests confirm the strong structural strength and excellent compressive properties of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F, which could ensure electrode integrity during the electrochemical reaction process. In addition, the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0003">3e</a> presents that the weight content of MoS<sub>2</sub> is 87.2%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0021_R_d90367811e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lots of MoS<sub>2</sub> monolayers with a sub-10&nbsp;nm lateral size (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7d</a>, Supporting Information) are embedded in the welded carbon networks, leading to good mechanical properties of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-Fs.</p>
            
            <p>The chemical states of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P were further investigated by Raman and XPS. According to Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a–c</a> (Supporting Information), the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F (3.46) is smaller than that of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P (4.00), indicating the smaller defect degree of carbon materials in MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0018_R_d90367811e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon Carbon">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result is in good agreement with the result in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0002">2e</a> (Supporting Information). Moreover, C 1s XPS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20d–f</a>, Supporting Information) further demonstrate the above result. The interfacial force between MoS<sub>2</sub> and carbon was studied by the Mo 3d and S 2p fine XPS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0003">3f,g</a>). As depicted in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the binding energies of Mo 3d and S 2p in MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F respectively present right shifts of 0.15 and 0.21&nbsp;eV, when compared with those in MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P. This suggests a strong interaction between MoS<sub>2</sub> and FC-F.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0022_R_d90367811e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307209-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202307209-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance of SMN/FC-Fs</h3>
            
            <p>The single-layer and sub-10&nbsp;nm lateral size of MoS<sub>2</sub>, the strong interfacial force between MoS<sub>2</sub> and carbon, and the free-standing porous network with excellent mechanical properties are greatly beneficial to making MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F has fast and stable reaction kinetics as anodes for alkali metal-ion batteries. To confirm this conjecture in SIBs, MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-Fs and FC-Fs were directly used as the anodes. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-Ps together with super-P carbon and sodium carboxyl-methyl cellulose (SCMC) were coated on the surface of Cu foil to construct a traditional anode. The cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307209-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information) exhibit that the MoS<sub>2</sub> contributes to the main sodium storage site. The reduction peak at 1.06&nbsp;V is assigned to the intercalation reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> into sub-10&nbsp;nm MoS<sub>2</sub> single-layer nanosheets and the formation of NaxMoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0023_R_d90367811e1189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Carbon">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The subsequent reduction peak at 0.8&nbsp;V is attributed to the conversion reaction (NaxMoS<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo) and the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0024_R_d90367811e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Nano">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> One broad oxidation peak at 1.78&nbsp;V corresponds to the reversible reaction of Mo/Na<sub>2</sub>S into MoS<sub>2</sub> in the first anodic scan.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0024_R_d90367811e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Nano">24</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0025_R_d90367811e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Environ. Sci.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The oxidation peak of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F at the first anodic scan is smaller than that of traditional MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P (1.83&nbsp;V), suggesting the enhanced reversible conversion reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0020_R_d90367811e1218" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0026_R_d90367811e1218" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the dQ/dV curves based on the initial discharge/charge profiles of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) also confirm the reversible conversion reaction mechanism. Moreover, the CV curves after the first cycle almost overlap, suggesting the durable conversion reaction reversibility of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0022_R_d90367811e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202307209-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5b042eb9-226e-42f3-b49a-924d53c03b63/adma202307209-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5b042eb9-226e-42f3-b49a-924d53c03b63/adma202307209-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2f89158a-40ac-4936-9bfc-a9ee68d7a8b6/adma202307209-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5b042eb9-226e-42f3-b49a-924d53c03b63/adma202307209-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307209-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307209" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of half and full cells for SIBs. a) CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V and b) GDC profiles at different current densities of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrodes. c) Rate capability and d) Cycling performance at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P. e) Cycling performances of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F at 44&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup>. f) Long-term cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Comparison of long-term cycling performance between MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and reported MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrodes. h) Cycling performances of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F/C//MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F full cell at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the initial 10 cycles and 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the rest cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Galvanostatic discharge–charge (GDC) measurements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0004">4b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information) from 0.1 to 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> were performed to study the rate capability. The capacity is calculated by the total weight of the whole electrode except Cu foil. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0004">4c</a>, with the current density increasing to 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode exhibits a high average discharge capacity of 258&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is 2.0 and 1.6 times those of the FC-F and traditional MoS2/PC-P electrodes, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The rate capabilities of MoS2/FC-F are higher than those of MoS2/PC-P and FC-F at all testing current densities. When the current density returns to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode achieves a high discharge capacity of 427&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than that of the traditional MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode (308&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and FC-F (285&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The above results indicate that MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode has excellent rate capability and highly robust structure even cycling after high current densities.</p>
            
            <p>The cycling performance was further studied to demonstrate the fast and stable reaction kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information), after cycling at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 10 cycles, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode shows a high discharge capacity of 392&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a high Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 95.11%. After 500 cycles, the specific capacity maintains a high value of 313&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a small capacity decay rate of 0.04% per cycle. For comparison, traditional MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode delivers a significantly smaller capacity of 165&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles with a faster capacity decay rate of 0.10% per cycle. Notably, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F has excellent compressive properties (demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0003">3d</a>), thus it also possesses a high-volume capacity of 26&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup> at 44&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup> after 500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0004">4e</a>). Moreover, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F also exhibits an excellent high-current long-term cycling performance. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0004">4f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information), after the initial 10 cycles cycling at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the MoS2/FC-F exhibits a high discharge capacity of 227&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than that of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P (168&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). After 2000 cycles, the reversible capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P is only kept up to 86&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a low-capacity retention of 51.1%. Interestingly, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode maintains a much higher capacity of 278&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with no capacity decay even after 5000 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This impressive high-current long-term cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F is better than those of mostly reported MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode materials at high current densities (≥1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) after long-life cycle (≥1000 cycles) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0004">4</a>&nbsp;g and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F half-cells exhibit excellent rate capability and high-current long-term cycling performance in SIBs, demonstrating the fast and stable reaction kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC- F. In addition, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F half-cells also exhibit high reversible capacities of 718 and 272&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 8 and 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 5000 and 4500 cycles with over 100% capacity retention in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs), respectively (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). This result further verifies the fast and stable reaction kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F, showing great potential in various EECSDs. In addition, Sn/FC-F and Fe3N/FC-F electrodes also display excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information). To further study the potential of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F in the practical application of SIBs, sodium ion full coin-cells composed of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F/C cathode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information) were assembled. The voltage window of the full cell was determined to be 1.0–4.1&nbsp;V based on CV curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29a</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in the cycling performance at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0004">4h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29b</a>, Supporting Information), the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F/C//MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F full cell exhibits a high reversible capacity of 185&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles, corresponding to a low-capacity decay rate of 0.05% per cycle. This result demonstrates that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F shows a good potential practical application.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307209-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202307209-sec-0060-title">2.4 Excellent Ion-Storage Kinetics of SMN/FC-Fs</h3>
            
            <p>To further discover the reasons for the outstanding electrochemical performances of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode, CV curves from 0.1 to 2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> were collected, as exhibited in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5a</a>. Generally, the charge storage behavior can be classified into two categories: faradaic and non-faradaic processes. The capacitive (non-faradaic) process shows high reaction kinetics. The <i>b</i>-value obtained from the relationship between current (<i>i</i>) and scanning rate (<i>v</i>) (<i>i</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>av<sup>b</sup></i>) could be used to evaluate the charge storage behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0005_R_d90367811e1528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Carbon Nat. Nanotechnol. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#adma202307209-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0027_R_d90367811e1528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Storage Mater. J. Energy Chem.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Normally, the value of <i>b</i> close to 1 represents a capacitive-controlled process, the <i>b</i> value near 0.5 suggests a diffused-controlled behavior. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> (Supporting Information), the <i>b</i> values of both MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes are close to 1, which indicates the capacitive-controlled process predominates the Na<sup>+</sup> storage. The capacitive-controlled contribution ratio could be quantitatively analyzed by <i>I</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>+<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup>, where, the <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup> represent capacitive-controlled behavior and diffusion-controlled behavior, respectively. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5b</a>, the CV curve of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode at 2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> can be divided into the dark-gray region (the diffusion control contribution) and the yellow region (capacitance control contribution), and the capacitive contribution ratio is 91.4%. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5c</a> depicts the normalized capacitive contribution percentage of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes at various scan rates. With the increasing scanning rate, the capacitive contribution percentage gradually increases, and the capacitive contribution percentage of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode is always higher than that of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode at all scanning rates. Therefore, MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F possesses a higher reaction kinetics, which is beneficial for excellent electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202307209-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/94880681-3996-4dd1-924a-049d13dec348/adma202307209-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/94880681-3996-4dd1-924a-049d13dec348/adma202307209-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/40a0865f-8662-424f-902c-d5770ef9ffca/adma202307209-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/94880681-3996-4dd1-924a-049d13dec348/adma202307209-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307209-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307209" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical reaction kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F for SIBs. a) CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode with various scan rates (0.1–2.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). b) Capacitive contribution (the yellow region) at a scan rate of 2.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Normalized capacitive contribution ratios of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes at various scan rates. d) Nyquist plot of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode after 100 cycles. e) <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values and f) <i>σ</i><sup>−2</sup> values of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes after different cycles. g) GITT profiles and corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients in the h) discharging and i) charging process of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To insight into the durable reaction kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F in SIBs, the electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) measurements were performed. After fitting the Nyquist plots of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P after different cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32a,b</a> Supporting Information), the reaction kinetics parameters of charge resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and Warburg coefficient (<i>σ</i>) can be calculated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0028_R_d90367811e1666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. J. Energy Chem. Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5e</a>, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode exhibits smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values than the traditional MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode in all testing states, suggesting faster electron transfer kinetics. The <i>σ</i> values are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32c,d</a> (Supporting Information). The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (DNa<sup>+</sup>) is positively correlated with <i>σ</i><sup>−2</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307209-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307209-bib-0028_R_d90367811e1695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. J. Energy Chem. Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5f</a>, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode exhibits larger <i>σ</i><sup>−2</sup> values than the traditional MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode in all testing states, suggesting larger Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Therefore, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode has better reaction kinetics than the traditional MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode in SIBs. Moreover, with increasing cycling numbers of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values show a trend of declining and then stabilizing, while the <i>σ</i><sup>−2</sup> values show a trend of rising and then stabilizing. Thus, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode shows durable Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics during repeated cycles.</p>
            
            <p>Moreover, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was employed to further analyze the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5g</a>. According to the GITT test results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a>, Supporting Information), the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5h,i</a>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5h</a>, the average Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode are 1.13&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−9</sup> and 1.07&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−9</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, during the discharge process. During the charge process, the average diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode were 1.54&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−9</sup> and 1.44&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−9</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307209-fig-0005">5i</a>), respectively. The obtained results further demonstrate that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode has larger average Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients than the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode in both discharging and charging processes. It is further demonstrated that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode has better electrochemical reaction kinetics than the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode.</p>
            
            <p>The durable Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F should be attributed to its excellent structural stability at both nanoscopic and macroscopic levels. After cycling 2000 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in SEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34a.b</a>, Supporting Information), the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode maintains free-standing morphology without structural cracking. The 3D continuous porous network structure is well maintained. They demonstrate that MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F has excellent stability at the macroscopic level. For comparison, several cracks appear in traditional MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35a</a>, Supporting Information). The 3D porous structure is collapsed and the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet derivatives are agglomerated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35b</a>, Supporting Information). On the other hand, as shown in the HRTEM image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34c</a>, Supporting Information), the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets keep the nanosheet structure even after repeated cycles, demonstrating the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F has excellent stability at the nanoscopic level. The highly reversible conversion reaction during repeated cycles is attributed to the good and stable reaction kinetics.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307209-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202307209-sec-0070-title">2.5 Discussion</h3>
            
            <p>Based on the above analysis, the advantages of sub-10&nbsp;nm MoS<sub>2</sub> monolayers embedded in free-standing porous carbon foams for enabling fast and stable reaction kinetics in SIB are illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307209-fig-0006">6</a>. For the traditional MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode, at the nanoscopic level, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics of large-size (&gt;300&nbsp;nm) multi-layer MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets suffer from low electrical conductivity and long Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion length. In addition, only one side of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets is anchored on the surface of carbon through weak interfacial force, leading to limited effect to prevent structural pulverization and aggregation during repeated cycles. On the other hand, the traditional powder electrode structure contains non-electrochemical active materials and shows random stacking, hindering electrolyte diffusion, electron transport, and structural integrity at the macroscopic level. The above two factors make the sodium conversion reaction kinetics in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrode sluggish and unstable, resulting in a slightly reversible conversion reaction of MoS<sub>2</sub> and rapid capacity decay. For the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F electrode, at the nanoscopic level, the sub-l0 nm MoS<sub>2</sub> monolayers greatly shorten the diffusion path of both electron and Na<sup>+</sup>. In addition, both sides of single-layer MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are embedded in carbon through strong interfacial force, resulting in reduced interfacial impedance, excellent structural stability, and weak shuttle effect. On the other hand, at the macroscopic level, the free-standing porous network structure has excellent mechanical properties, providing rapid diffusion channels for electrons and electrolytes and keeping the structural integrity of the electrode structure. Combining both advantages at the nanoscopic and macroscopic levels, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F has fast and stable reaction kinetics, resulting in a highly reversible conversion reaction and superior long-term cycling performance even at high current densities.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202307209-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a2decee9-e540-48ec-a909-d3f6aab455e4/adma202307209-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a2decee9-e540-48ec-a909-d3f6aab455e4/adma202307209-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0197c5ed-4647-4e78-a322-57a6331e81a1/adma202307209-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a2decee9-e540-48ec-a909-d3f6aab455e4/adma202307209-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307209-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307209" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Scheme for conversion reaction mechanisms of MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-F and MoS<sub>2</sub>/PC-P electrodes during cycling.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202307209-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202307209-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a universal salt template crossing with the powder metallurgy method is successfully developed to prepare a series of SMN/FC-Fs. The pressure-enhanced interfacial confinement of the NaCl self-assembly not only promotes defect healing and interfacial welding between carbon nanosheets but also blocks the diffusion and agglomeration of SMNs embedded in carbon layers, which has been demonstrated by combining advanced characterization technologies with theoretical calculations. As a result, SMNs and FC-Fs are in situ synchronously formed at the same temperature of 650&nbsp;°C. The prepared SMN/FC-Fs have a free-standing structure, excellent mechanical properties, a porous network structure, good electrical conductivity, and strong interfacial interaction. The unique structural advantages at the nanoscopic and macroscopic levels enable fast and stable ion storage reaction kinetics. Taking MoS<sub>2</sub>/FC-Fs as a proof-of-concept demonstration, they have highly reversible conversion reactions in SIBs. As a result, they exhibit superior long-term cycling performance as both half-cell and full-cell. This study opens up new opportunities to design and prepare emerging and promising SMN-based electrodes for applications in EECSDs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
